Real Estate
Foreign Cue
503 words
24 December 2008
The Wall Street Journal (Online and Print)
WSJO
Real Estate; C8
English
Copyright 2008 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

Across the globe this December, funds that invest government money have been among the few buyers of big-ticket properties.

On Tuesday, cash-strapped U.S. warehouse giant ProLogis announced it would sell many of its Asian assets for $1.3 billion to affiliates of GIC Real Estate, Singapore's government-owned real-estate investment company. And earlier this month, the Libyan Investment Authority closed a $176 million deal to acquire an office building in London, according to research firm Real Capital Analytics.

Is foreign money ready to lead the way back into real estate?

Raymond Torto, chief economist at commercial brokerage CB Richard Ellis, says he is talking to "a whole bunch of folks" who are seeking to allocate billions more to real estate. He mentioned one Mexican investor prepared to commit as much as $3 billion in cash to real estate around the world. Of course there's no guarantee foreign investors -- most of whom have their own problems -- will start buying U.S. property anytime soon.

Anton Troianovski

Providence Place for Sale

Beleaguered mall owner General Growth Properties Inc. has put another top asset on the block to help it pare its $27 billion debt load: Providence Place in Providence, R.I.

The upscale mall, located in downtown Providence across the street from the Rhode Island Convention Center, registers hardy sales per square foot of $560 and occupancy of more than 96%. It is being marketed by New York brokerage Savills Granite LLC. It is one of at least 10 malls General Growth is trying to sell as it negotiates with lenders to extend debt-payment deadlines. Finding a buyer in this capital-scarce market will be challenging. "It's a solid property, as many on the market are right now," said Greg Maloney, chief executive of Jones Lang LaSalle's U.S. retail division. "Once capital frees up and we can determine the cost of capital, then we will see properties start to trade."

Kris Hudson

Commercial Expansion

Having survived the residential-mortgage crunch, Annaly Capital Management Inc. is looking to expand into the down-on-its-luck commercial-property-debt market.

The mortgage real-estate investment trust was one of the most conservative -- and successful -- when it came to investing in residential-mortgage securities, using relatively low leverage and avoiding toxic subprime assets. Most REITs specializing in residential mortgages have been clobbered by the credit-market crisis. Annaly invests in federal-agency-guaranteed securities backed by home loans.

Now the New York firm's investment advisory unit, Fidac, is exploring raising capital to invest in debt used to finance office towers, shopping malls, hotels and other types of commercial property. "There will be more stress on real estate, but we believe we have the ability to harvest value from the situations," says Kevin J. Riordan, who was hired by Annaly to lead the initiative. He is the former head of commercial-mortgage securities for TIAA-CREF, the largest U.S. retirement system.

Lingling Wei

Dow Jones & Company, Inc.

Leading the News: U.S. home sales plunge while prices keep falling
By Jessica Holzer and Jeff Bater
802 words
24 December 2008
The Wall Street Journal Asia
AWSJ
2
English
(c) 2008 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. To see the edition in which this article appeared, click here http://awsj.com.hk/factiva-ns[http://awsj.com.hk/factiva-ns]

WASHINGTON -- Sales of both new and existing homes in the U.S. tumbled in November, and prices continued to fall, as a plunging stock market and a sharply deteriorating economy drove consumers out of the market.

Sales of existing homes, which include single-family homes and condos, fell 8.6% to a seasonally adjusted annual rate of 4.49 million units in November, a 10.6% drop from a year ago, the National Association of Realtors reported Tuesday.

Meanwhile, sales of existing single-family homes plunged 8% to a seasonally adjusted annual rate of 4.02 million in November, representing the lowest sales activity since July 1997.

The national median home price fell to $181,300, a decline of 13.2% from a year ago, and the largest drop since the trade group began its survey in 1968. Lawrence Yun, the trade group's chief economist, said it was likely the largest decline since the Great Depression.

The total inventory of unsold homes rose 0.1% to 4.2 million in November, the highest since the 1980s. This represents a supply of 11.2 months, up from 10.3 months in October.

Mr. Yun attributed the weakness to the softening economy and the stock-market plunge last month. He argued that Congress should act to shore up home prices by providing tax incentives to home buyers.

New-home sales fell a fourth time in a row during November, and prices remained below year-earlier levels.

Sales of single-family homes decreased 2.9% to a seasonally adjusted annual rate of 407,000, the Commerce Department said Tuesday. October new-home sales fell 5.2% to an annual rate of 419,000; originally, the government said October sales retreated 5.3% to 433,000. Sales fell 1.3% in September and 11.3% in August.

Economists surveyed by Dow Jones Newswires had called for a 4.2% drop in November sales to a 415,000 annual rate.

New-home sales were 35.3% lower than the level in November 2007.

The Commerce report showed there were an estimated 374,000 homes for sale at the end of November, representing 11.5 months' supply at the current sales rate. In October, an estimated 402,000 were for sale, an 11.8-month inventory.

The median price of a new home plunged 11.5% to $220,400 in November from $249,100 in November 2007. The average price decreased 9.2% to $287,500 from $316,800 a year earlier. In October this year, the median price was $214,600 and the average was $279,500.

Regionally last month, new-home sales fell 7.1% in the South and 16.4% in the Midwest. Sales rose 11.0% in the West and 14.3% in the Northeast.

An estimated 28,000 homes were actually sold in November, down from 33,000 in October, based on figures not seasonally adjusted.

Meanwhile, the U.S. government left its estimate for the summer's economic deterioration unchanged, according to data Tuesday that exceeded the expectations of Wall Street analysts.

Gross domestic product fell at a 0.5% annual rate July through September, the Commerce Department reported. That was the same rate reported a month earlier.

GDP is a measure of all goods and services produced in the economy.

Wall Street had expected a slight downward adjustment to third-quarter GDP; economists surveyed by Dow Jones Newswires saw a revised decline of 0.6%.

The 0.5% slide in third-quarter GDP marked the biggest decrease since a 1.4% decline in the third quarter of 2001. Economists think that GDP in the current quarter fell far deeper, perhaps by as much as 6%. The first government estimate for GDP in that period, which ends next week, is due to be released in about a month. The rule of thumb is the economy is in recession when GDP declines for two consecutive quarters. But the National Bureau of Economic Research has already declared recession. The nonprofit group of academics monitors the U.S. business cycle and announced this month that a recession began a year ago.

Gauges measuring third-quarter price inflation were adjusted to show that prices didn't rise as much as earlier thought, according to Tuesday's data revisions.

Corporate profits after taxes were revised a bit lower. After-tax earnings fell 3.2% to $1.3 trillion in July through September from the second quarter, the report showed. Profits previously were estimated falling 3%. Second-quarter earnings decreased 0.4%. Year over year, profits fell 10.1% since the third quarter of 2007.

Ahead of the Tape
Markets
Seeking Signs of a Recovery for Housing
By Mark Gongloff
532 words
23 December 2008
The Wall Street Journal (Online and Print)
WSJO
Markets; C1
English
Copyright 2008 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

Just when it seemed nearer, the housing recovery has darted away again.

On Tuesday the National Association of Realtors reports November home resales and the Commerce Department reports November new-home sales. Economists expect both measures fell during a month when economic activity generally came to a screeching halt.

Home resales, the bulk of the market, had seemed to stabilize this fall. But that was largely because of brisk foreclosure sales in stressed markets. And this thin silver lining has dimmed: Pending home sales, a leading resale indicator, have fallen for two months in a row after rising to their 2008 peak this summer.

A nagging oversupply of houses and record-low home-builder confidence suggest new-home sales won't perk up soon, either. There are hopeful signs for housing, critical to the health of the broader economy. Years of falling prices have made houses intriguingly affordable, especially relative to rising rents.

And mortgage rates have tumbled to their lowest levels in nearly 40 years. Barclays Capital economists estimate that, since 1975, one percentage point of falling borrowing costs has added 15% to home sales and 1.5% to home prices.

They warn, however, that those results depended on financial markets functioning "more or less normally." Not so now. With credit still tight, unemployment rising and household balance sheets still debt-heavy, lower rates have so far mainly produced more refinancings, not sales. Slashing rates to 4.5% for new borrowers, as the government aims to do, won't be a panacea, either. Ivy Zelman, chief executive of housing-research firm Zelman &Associates, estimates that, even with such a low rate, only about 67% of U.S. households can afford a house. Homeownership was nearly 68% in the third quarter, according to the Census Bureau, implying there is virtually no untapped demand for homes.

Card Firm Has Tidings; Comfort, Joy Doubtful

Don't expect much cheer in the card American Greetings is sending to investors this holiday season.

The greeting-cards firm reports third-quarter results Tuesday, and if the past two quarters are any indication, the numbers could be as bleak as today's national psyche. In the first two quarters of the current fiscal year, the firm missed analysts' estimates by more than 50%.

Only two analysts have published estimates for the third quarter, and they expect American Greetings will earn, on average, 52 cents a share -- though the range is exceedingly wide at 44 cents to 60 cents. A year ago, American Greetings earned 53 cents a share. Just where the numbers land will depend partly on cost-savings initiatives the company announced earlier this month.

Analysts have trimmed revenue and earnings forecasts through fiscal 2010. Moreover, in recent reports both Stein Research and Gabelli &Co. expressed concern dysfunctional credit markets might have mucked up the company's $195 million sale of its Care Bears and Strawberry Shortcake properties, which was supposed to close in September but still hasn't. Analysts will seek guidance on that as well.

Jeff D. Opdyke
